data-tracker-events="click" data-tracker-action="click" data-tracker-category="nav" data-tracker-label="article.topic" class="" href="/web/20160505185403/http://www.bloombergview.com/topics/investing" data-reactid=".fmr69s7q4g.0.3.0.3.$O61PQN6TTDSC01.0.0.0">Investing</a></div><h1 class="_3xbtCFgeFFEEkPNK99UFwD" data-reactid=".fmr69s7q4g.0.3.0.3.$O61PQN6TTDSC01.0.1"><span data-reactid=".fmr69s7q4g.0.3.0.3.$O61PQN6TTDSC01.0.1.0">Overpriced Index Funds Won't Go Away</span></h1><section class="_2uup1KAgNGCuZ7okk3cdrp _1Agnf6oYPshxF271GUtf3U" data-reactid=".fmr69s7q4g.0.3.0.3.$O61PQN6TTDSC01.0.2"><div class="D-6TRly7uF-GAAG3CF5kN _2BCDyx14zK3VePp-3Ncqn_" data-reactid=".fmr69s7q4g.0.3.0.3.$O61PQN6TTDSC01.0.2.1"><div class="_1NXXsxMAW-qEkusnFnpdta" data-reactid=".fmr69s7q4g.0.3.0.3.$O61PQN6TTDSC01.0.2.1.0"><div class="" data-tracker-label="story.contributor.headshot" data-reactid=".fmr69s7q4g.0.3.0.3.$O61PQN6TTDSC01.0.2.1.0.0"><img class="_2SGXSP5Ibqw9rm3LQ3YUM_" src="//web.archive.org/web/20160505185403im_/http://assets.bwbx.io/images/i1JWQKZPC96A/v1/120x120.jpg" data-reactid=".fmr69s7q4g.0.3.0.3.$O61PQN6TTDSC01.0.2.1.0.0.0"/></div></div><div class="_849R9bR0SWtXY4QWZ4mfh" data-reactid=".fmr69s7q4g.0.3.0.3.$O61PQN6TTDSC01.0.2.1.1"><span class="Dychel1w-Ejah3VeMQsif" data-reactid=".fmr69s7q4g.0.3.0.3.$O61PQN6TTDSC01.0.2.1.1.0">Barry Ritholtz is a Bloomberg View columnist writing about finance, the economy and the business world. He started the Big Picture blog in 2003 and is the founder of Ritholtz Wealth Management, an asset management and financial planning firm.</span><br data-reactid=".fmr69s7q4g.0.3.0.3.$O61PQN6TTDSC01.0.2.1.1.1"/><a class="_1k-qXSyhl--aqegJ8Z__-c" data-tracker-label="article.author.article_list" data-tracker-category="nav" data-tracker-action="click" data-tracker-events="click" href="/web/20160505185403/http://www.bloombergview.com/contributors/ABrfppB0vtA/michael-lewis" data-reactid=".fmr69s7q4g.0.3.0.3.$O61PQN6TTDSC01.0.2.1.1.2">Read More.</a><div class="_9oHlKQGyJJLYUvh6vlbie social" data-tracker-events="click" data-tracker-category="social" data-tracker-action="follow" data-tracker-label="article.twitter" data-reactid=".fmr69s7q4g.0.3.0.3.$O61PQN6TTDSC01.0.2.1.1.3"><a href="http://web.archive.org/web/20160505185403/https://twitter.com/ritholtz" class="twitter-follow-button" data-show-count="false" data-dnt="true" data-reactid=".fmr69s7q4g.0.3.0.3.$O61PQN6TTDSC01.0.2.1.1.3.0"><span data-reactid=".fmr69s7q4g.0.3.0.3.$O61PQN6TTDSC01.0.2.1.1.3.0.0">@</span><span data-reactid=".fmr69s7q4g.0.3.0.3.$O61PQN6TTDSC01.0.2.1.1.3.0.1">ritholtz</span></a></div></div></div></section><div class="WmLiANu2di5ImYD1nZILR" data-reactid=".fmr69s7q4g.0.3.0.3.$O61PQN6TTDSC01.0.3"><div class="_1aYLwgagu_KTy6L1T5rxky oSD1Gt7fG3I7i4Sp6lJIk" data-reactid=".fmr69s7q4g.0.3.0.3.$O61PQN6TTDSC01.0.3.1"><time class="Hzw8hyP5BimGTuKF0h2Sz" datetime="2016-04-22T17:25:35.861Z" data-reactid=".fmr69s7q4g.0.3.0.3.$O61PQN6TTDSC01.0.3.1.0">April 22, 2016 1:25 PM EST</time></div></div><div class="_8jbprFjVaGulwqSz9fyst" data-reactid=".fmr69s7q4g.0.3.0.3.$O61PQN6TTDSC01.0.4"><div class="ShkfDz8Jp4pWbEeSLDyqV VaGqsw9REMl610LD48_ps" data-reactid=".fmr69s7q4g.0.3.0.3.$O61PQN6TTDSC01.0.4.0"><a class="_2pJt_Ch3L0py-aTSNdUmkg" data-reactid=".fmr69s7q4g.0.3.0.3.$O61PQN6TTDSC01.0.4.0.0">a</a><span data-reactid=".fmr69s7q4g.0.3.0.3.$O61PQN6TTDSC01.0.4.0.1"> | </span><a class="_1ON5PSfFPoiVcUbNDoq2tw" data-reactid=".fmr69s7q4g.0.3.0.3.$O61PQN6TTDSC01.0.4.0.2">A</a></div></div><div class="_3XSGr6I3NQnUqNfGgvwoyX" data-reactid=".fmr69s7q4g.0.3.0.3.$O61PQN6TTDSC01.0.5"><span class="_2b5AlwPvzNLmZWlKrK1qBv" data-reactid=".fmr69s7q4g.0.3.0.3.$O61PQN6TTDSC01.0.5.0">By</span><div class=" byline" data-reactid=".fmr69s7q4g.0.3.0.3.$O61PQN6TTDSC01.0.5.1"><a data-tracker-label="article.byline" data-tracker-action="click" data-tracker-events="click" data-tracker-category="nav" rel="author" class="" href="/web/20160505185403/http://www.bloombergview.com/contributors/AEq0WJRomcY/barry-l-ritholtz" data-reactid=".fmr69s7q4g.0.3.0.3.$O61PQN6TTDSC01.0.5.1.$AEq0WJRomcY/barry-l-ritholtz">Barry Ritholtz</a></div></div></div><div class="_31WvjDF17ltgFb1fNB1WqY" data-reactid=".fmr69s7q4g.0.3.0.3.$O61PQN6TTDSC01.1"><p>Many traders and investors acts as if markets are efficient, meaning that asset prices fully reflect all available information. We see this manifest itself in numerous ways -- how prices rapidly adjust to new information and how few investors manage to beat the broader indexes by picking stocks or trying to time the market.</p><p>At least that&apos;s the gist of the efficient market hypothesis, which won University of Chicago economist Eugene Fama a Nobel Prize. The problem is that in practice, there are lots of information asymmetries and frictions that make markets somewhat less than perfectly efficient. To reflect this, I started referring to Fama&#x2019;s thesis as the <a href="http://web.archive.org/web/20160505185403/http://www.ritholtz.com/blog/2004/11/the-kinda-eventually-sorta-mostly-almost-efficient-market-theory/" data-web-url="http://www.ritholtz.com/blog/2004/11/the-kinda-eventually-sorta-mostly-almost-efficient-market-theory/">kinda-eventually-sorta-mostly-almost efficient market theory</a>.</p><p>That description leads us to today&#x2019;s discussion about high-priced index funds<em>.</em></p><p>If that sounds sort of contradictory, that&#x2019;s because <em>it is</em>. One of the key ideas behind index funds is that they have low costs, turnover and taxes. Since most investors can&apos;t beat the markets, why not just match the market in a fund that tracks a major index and keep investment and management expenses to a bare minimum.</p><p>But that isn&apos;t always the case. While many index funds have fees that are almost 100 basis points less than actively managed funds, according to the <a href="http://web.archive.org/web/20160505185403/http://www.wsj.com/articles/dont-assume-index-funds-will-always-save-you-money-1460712607" data-web-url="http://www.wsj.com/articles/dont-assume-index-funds-will-always-save-you-money-1460712607">Wall Street Journal</a>, there still are quite a few expensive index funds out there. Michael Johnston of <a href="http://web.archive.org/web/20160505185403/http://fundreference.com/articles/2015/1000754/best-s-and-p-500-index-fund/" data-web-url="http://fundreference.com/articles/2015/1000754/best-s-and-p-500-index-fund/">Fund Reference</a> lists 94 funds that track the Standard &amp; Poor&apos;s 500 Index, sorted by expense ratio and minimum investment. Johnston concludes that only three of the funds are worth bothering with.</p><p>The most egregious example cited in his list: the $275 million Rydex S&amp;P 500 fund, with annual expenses for the C class shares at a whopping 2.32 percent. Compare that with the Fidelity Spartan 500 Index Advantage fund and Vanguard 500 Index Admiral fund, both of which charge 0.05 percent. The other fund that gets Johnston&apos;s thumbs up is the Schwab S&amp;P 500 Index, with annual charges of 0.09 percent, and a minimum opening investment of just $100.</p><p>More to the point, the real test of an investment is what you get for your money. As the chart below shows, the Rydex S&amp;P 500 fund&apos;s high fees lead to underperformance.</p><figure data-type="image" data-id="258194576" data-thumbnail="//assets.bwbx.io/images/i.3Tr0_6lQX0/v1/460x260.png" align="center" data-align="center"><div class="container"></div><figcaption><div class="news-figure-caption-text"></div></figcaption></figure><p>Since 2011, the fund has returned an annual 8.5 percent, while the S&amp;P 500 itself has gained 11 percent a year, according to data compiled by Bloomberg.</p><p>So how can we explain why people waste so much money on a simple index fund? Shouldn&#x2019;t the market correct this?</p><p>Eventually, the efficient market hypothesis goes, it should. But it seems to take a whole lot longer than the theory would imply. Keep in mind, there are number of significant factors working against the average retail investor: Investing itself can be complex and time-consuming, and many people find it confusing. Note that this is a feature of the industry, not a bug. If everything was simple and understandable and free from jargon, high-fee index funds wouldn&apos;t exist.</p><p>Despite this being the age of Google search, individual investors confront a tremendous information asymmetry. The industry knows much more than the consumer; brokers are aware of what things should cost and how much they can get away with charging investors. Let&apos;s be honest: Sales people understand and make money off of impulses and irrational behavior. This goes a long way toward answering why theories that count on rational market actors often fail.</p><p>In turn, this might help to explain why expensive index funds stay in business.</p><p>Another Nobel Prize winner, Paul Krugman, tried to explain market inefficiencies in a <a href="http://web.archive.org/web/20160505185403/http://archive.fortune.com/magazines/fortune/fortune_archive/1997/12/29/235886/index.htm" data-web-url="http://archive.fortune.com/magazines/fortune/fortune_archive/1997/12/29/235886/index.htm">1997 Fortune article</a>. He identified seven bad habits or attitudes of investors that make markets less&#xA0;efficient:</p><p>&#x2022; Think short term.</p><p>&#x2022; Be greedy.</p><p>&#x2022; Believe in the greater fool.</p><p>&#x2022; Run with the herd.</p><p>&#x2022; Overgeneralize.</p><p>&#x2022; Be trendy.</p><p>&#x2022; Play with other people&#x2019;s money.</p><p>All of this introduces huge amounts of inefficiency to markets. None of these behavioral patterns show any sign of disappearing soon.</p><p>As noted before, markets eventually get it more or less correct, but along the way, they can deviate from the true path of efficiency. We just need to wait a decade or three for that efficiency to sort itself out.</p><p class="news-disclaimer-text">This column does not necessarily reflect the opinion of the editorial board or Bloomberg LP and its owners.</p><p>To contact the author of this story:<br><span itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">    <meta itemprop="url" content="http://web.archive.org/web/20160505185403/bbg://people/profile/4895832">    <span itemprop="name">        Barry Ritholtz    </span></span> at <a href="http://web.archive.org/web/20160505185403/mailto:britholtz3@bloomberg.net">britholtz3@bloomberg.net</a></p><p>To contact the editor responsible for this story:<br><span itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">    <meta itemprop="url" content="http://web.archive.org/web/20160505185403/bbg://people/profile/1708358">    <span itemprop="name">        James Greiff     </span></span> at <a href="http://web.archive.org/web/20160505185403/mailto:jgreiff@bloomberg.net">jgreiff@bloomberg.net</a></p></div><div class="_1uoMHb1BEtMQ3QpFNVdGUn" data-reactid=".fmr69s7q4g.0.3.0.3.$O61PQN6TTDSC01.2"><ul class="_3lAThsPbDH9w6aXcskd8Yq _39hK9wfVsGB_6IMnXsBGFw" data-tracker-events="click" data-tracker-category="social" data-tracker-action="share" data-reactid=".fmr69s7q4g.0.3.0.3.$O61PQN6TTDSC01.2.0"><li class="_2HYFQd9zXPWcbKTaZhFLEY" data-tracker-label="article.bottom.share_buttons.facebook" data-reactid=".fmr69s7q4g.0.3.0.3.$O61PQN6TTDSC01.2.0.3"><span data-reactid=".fmr69s7q4g.0.3.0.3.$O61PQN6TTDSC01.2.0.3.0">Print</span></li></ul></div></article><aside class="_3m2nXnJshfQ4w6fzX9WjHy" data-reactid=".fmr69s7q4g.0.3.0.3.2.1"><h1 class="_2pegX1lWRonznG6XMjZi4B" data-reactid=".fmr69s7q4g.0.3.0.3.2.1.0">Most Popular</h1><ul data-reactid=".fmr69s7q4g.0.3.0.3.2.1.1"><li class="_28wp1UFysWPevaE2Pv1xos" data-tracker-events="click" data-tracker-category="nav" data-tracker-action="click" data-tracker-label="article.popular.story.01" data-reactid=".fmr69s7q4g.0.3.0.3.2.1.1.$popular-article-O6P5106JTSED01"><article class="_2G6c-PErBAitUD2wsS733r" data-reactid=".fmr69s7q4g.0.3.0.3.2.1.1.$popular-article-O6P5106JTSED01.0"><div He lives on New York's Long Island with his wife.","photoUrl":"\u002F\u002Fweb.archive.org\u002Fweb\u002F20160505185403\u002Fhttp:\u002F\u002Fassets.bwbx.io\u002Fimages\u002Fi1JWQKZPC96A\u002Fv1\u002F120x120.jpg"},"id":"O61PQN6TTDSC01","slug":"articles\u002F2016-04-22\u002Fthe-mystery-of-why-costly-index-funds-survive","headline":{"text":"Overpriced Index Funds Won't Go Away","html":"Overpriced Index Funds Won't Go Away"},"publishedAt":"2016-04-22T17:25:35.861Z","url":"http:\u002F\u002Fweb.archive.org\u002Fweb\u002F20160505185403\u002Fhttp:\u002F\u002Fwww.bloombergview.com\u002Farticles\u002F2016-04-22\u002Fthe-mystery-of-why-costly-index-funds-survive","shortenedUrl":"http:\u002F\u002Fweb.archive.org\u002Fweb\u002F20160505185403\u002Fhttp:\u002F\u002Fbv.ms\u002F1WKIvSm","adTargeting":{"suid":"O61PQN6TTDSC01","page":"article","currentResource":"Story|O61PQN6TTDSC01","ni":["ALLFND","ALLTOP","ALLWWCUR","BBVCOL","BIZNEWS","BUSINESS","BVIEW","BVIEWTEAM","BVTOP","BVTOPZ3","BVTOPZ4","BVTOPZ5","BVTOPZ6","BVTOPZ7","BVTOPZ8","COMTOP","COS","ECO","FIN","MISC","FLOWCMS","FND","GOWEB","HLNOVEL","HLNOVELTY9","MARKETS","NOVEL","NOVELTY9","NYX","ONWEB","OPINION","ORIGINAL","READ","READ100","READ150","READ25","READ250","READ50","READ500","READ700","RITHOLTZ","SENT","STK","USBNX","WORLD","ATTACHMENT","BBPROFILE","BGOVBILLGO","BGOVCODES","BIOINSDM","BNALL","BNSTAFF","BNTEAMS","TIMENI","EQUITYKEY","TIMECO","SRCRANK5","SPREGIONS","PHOTO","INTERNAL","MAJOR","FINNEWS","INDUSTRIES","METRO","MSCIWORLD","NOBEL","NORTHAM","PADD2","PADDIST","MSCINAMER","IL","G7MEMB","G10MEMB","CHICAGO","US","USMW","ALLSTATES"],"tagr":[]},"metadata":{"flowcmsId":401152,"thumbnail":"258194778","background":"white","halfTile":false,"suppressComments":true,"seo":{"headline":"The Mystery of Why Costly Index Funds Survive"},"googleStandout":false,"social":{"twitterText":"Barry Ritholtz: Overpriced index funds won't go away","twitterTitle":"","twitterDescription":"","twitterImage":0,"facebookStatus":"Don't confuse me with the facts.","headline":"","description":"","image":0},"ledeTreatment":"","lede":{"type":"image","id":258194778,"caption":"\u003Cp\u003EJust early.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n","credit":"Photographer: Scott Olsen\u002FGetty Images","overlay":""},"adCode":"","disableAds":false,"suppressRecirc":false,"magazine":false},"type":"article","byline":"Barry Ritholtz","body":"\u003Cp\u003EMany traders and investors acts as if markets are efficient, meaning that asset prices fully reflect all available information. We see this manifest itself in numerous ways -- how prices rapidly adjust to new information and how few investors manage to beat the broader indexes by picking stocks or trying to time the market.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EAt least that&apos;s the gist of the efficient market hypothesis, which won University of Chicago economist Eugene Fama a Nobel Prize. The problem is that in practice, there are lots of information asymmetries and frictions that make markets somewhat less than perfectly efficient. To reflect this, I started referring to Fama&#x2019;s thesis as the \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.ritholtz.com\u002Fblog\u002F2004\u002F11\u002Fthe-kinda-eventually-sorta-mostly-almost-efficient-market-theory\u002F\" data-web-url=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.ritholtz.com\u002Fblog\u002F2004\u002F11\u002Fthe-kinda-eventually-sorta-mostly-almost-efficient-market-theory\u002F\"\u003Ekinda-eventually-sorta-mostly-almost efficient market theory\u003C\u002Fa\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThat description leads us to today&#x2019;s discussion about high-priced index funds\u003Cem\u003E.\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EIf that sounds sort of contradictory, that&#x2019;s because \u003Cem\u003Eit is\u003C\u002Fem\u003E. One of the key ideas behind index funds is that they have low costs, turnover and taxes. Since most investors can&apos;t beat the markets, why not just match the market in a fund that tracks a major index and keep investment and management expenses to a bare minimum.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EBut that isn&apos;t always the case. While many index funds have fees that are almost 100 basis points less than actively managed funds, according to the \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.wsj.com\u002Farticles\u002Fdont-assume-index-funds-will-always-save-you-money-1460712607\" data-web-url=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.wsj.com\u002Farticles\u002Fdont-assume-index-funds-will-always-save-you-money-1460712607\"\u003EWall Street Journal\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, there still are quite a few expensive index funds out there. Michael Johnston of \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Ffundreference.com\u002Farticles\u002F2015\u002F1000754\u002Fbest-s-and-p-500-index-fund\u002F\" data-web-url=\"http:\u002F\u002Ffundreference.com\u002Farticles\u002F2015\u002F1000754\u002Fbest-s-and-p-500-index-fund\u002F\"\u003EFund Reference\u003C\u002Fa\u003E lists 94 funds that track the Standard &amp; Poor&apos;s 500 Index, sorted by expense ratio and minimum investment. Johnston concludes that only three of the funds are worth bothering with.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe most egregious example cited in his list: the $275 million Rydex S&amp;P 500 fund, with annual expenses for the C class shares at a whopping 2.32 percent. Compare that with the Fidelity Spartan 500 Index Advantage fund and Vanguard 500 Index Admiral fund, both of which charge 0.05 percent. The other fund that gets Johnston&apos;s thumbs up is the Schwab S&amp;P 500 Index, with annual charges of 0.09 percent, and a minimum opening investment of just $100.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EMore to the point, the real test of an investment is what you get for your money. As the chart below shows, the Rydex S&amp;P 500 fund&apos;s high fees lead to underperformance.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cfigure data-type=\"image\" data-id=\"258194576\" data-thumbnail=\"\u002F\u002Fassets.bwbx.io\u002Fimages\u002Fi.3Tr0_6lQX0\u002Fv1\u002F460x260.png\" align=\"center\" data-align=\"center\"\u003E\u003Cdiv class=\"container\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\u003Cfigcaption\u003E\u003Cdiv class=\"news-figure-caption-text\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E\u003C\u002Ffigcaption\u003E\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E\u003Cp\u003ESince 2011, the fund has returned an annual 8.5 percent, while the S&amp;P 500 itself has gained 11 percent a year, according to data compiled by Bloomberg.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003ESo how can we explain why people waste so much money on a simple index fund? Shouldn&#x2019;t the market correct this?\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EEventually, the efficient market hypothesis goes, it should. But it seems to take a whole lot longer than the theory would imply. Keep in mind, there are number of significant factors working against the average retail investor: Investing itself can be complex and time-consuming, and many people find it confusing. Note that this is a feature of the industry, not a bug. If everything was simple and understandable and free from jargon, high-fee index funds wouldn&apos;t exist.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EDespite this being the age of Google search, individual investors confront a tremendous information asymmetry. The industry knows much more than the consumer; brokers are aware of what things should cost and how much they can get away with charging investors. Let&apos;s be honest: Sales people understand and make money off of impulses and irrational behavior. This goes a long way toward answering why theories that count on rational market actors often fail.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EIn turn, this might help to explain why expensive index funds stay in business.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EAnother Nobel Prize winner, Paul Krugman, tried to explain market inefficiencies in a \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Farchive.fortune.com\u002Fmagazines\u002Ffortune\u002Ffortune_archive\u002F1997\u002F12\u002F29\u002F235886\u002Findex.htm\" data-web-url=\"http:\u002F\u002Farchive.fortune.com\u002Fmagazines\u002Ffortune\u002Ffortune_archive\u002F1997\u002F12\u002F29\u002F235886\u002Findex.htm\"\u003E1997 Fortune article\u003C\u002Fa\u003E. He identified seven bad habits or attitudes of investors that make markets less&#xA0;efficient:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E&#x2022; Think short term.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E&#x2022; Be greedy.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E&#x2022; Believe in the greater fool.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E&#x2022; Run with the herd.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E&#x2022; Overgeneralize.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E&#x2022; Be trendy.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E&#x2022; Play with other people&#x2019;s money.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EAll of this introduces huge amounts of inefficiency to markets. None of these behavioral patterns show any sign of disappearing soon.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EAs noted before, markets eventually get it more or less correct, but along the way, they can deviate from the true path of efficiency. We just need to wait a decade or three for that efficiency to sort itself out.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp class=\"news-disclaimer-text\"\u003EThis column does not necessarily reflect the opinion of the editorial board or Bloomberg LP and its owners.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003ETo contact the author of this story:\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cspan itemscope=\"itemscope\" itemtype=\"http:\u002F\u002Fschema.org\u002FPerson\"\u003E    \u003Cmeta itemprop=\"url\" content=\"bbg:\u002F\u002Fpeople\u002Fprofile\u002F4895832\"\u003E    \u003Cspan itemprop=\"name\"\u003E        Barry Ritholtz    \u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E at \u003Ca href=\"mailto:britholtz3@bloomberg.net\"\u003Ebritholtz3@bloomberg.net\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003ETo contact the editor responsible for this story:\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cspan itemscope=\"itemscope\" itemtype=\"http:\u002F\u002Fschema.org\u002FPerson\"\u003E    \u003Cmeta itemprop=\"url\" content=\"bbg:\u002F\u002Fpeople\u002Fprofile\u002F1708358\"\u003E    \u003Cspan itemprop=\"name\"\u003E        James Greiff     \u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E at \u003Ca href=\"mailto:jgreiff@bloomberg.net\"\u003Ejgreiff@bloomberg.net\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E","absoluteUrl":"\u002Farticles\u002F2016-04-22\u002Fthe-mystery-of-why-costly-index-funds-survive","summary":"Markets aren't quite as efficient as academic theory suggests.","ledeImage":{"baseUrl":"\u002F\u002Fweb.archive.org\u002Fweb\u002F20160505185403\u002Fhttp:\u002F\u002Fassets.bwbx.io\u002Fimages\u002Fifekyqvhd8kA\u002Fv1\u002F-1x-1.jpg","description":"CHICAGO, IL - OCTOBER 14:  University of Chicago professor Eugene Fama addresses colllegues, students and media at the university after learning he had won the Nobel Prize in Economic Sciences on October 14, 2013 in Chicago, Illinois. Ritholtz"],"keywords":[]},"meta":{"ogTitle":"Overpriced Index Funds Won't Go Away","schemaTitle":"Overpriced Index Funds Won't Go Away","description":"Markets aren't quite as efficient as academic
